PAPUA NEW GUINEA
POPONDETTA SMALLHOLDER OIL PALM DEVELOPMET PROJECT
Draft Terms of Reference for Agricultural Credit Specialist
1. Objectives
The objectives are (a) to help DPI and PNGDB to improve their per-
formance in smallholder credit operat:ions in agriculture and (b) assist in
the training of DPI and PNGDB field staff for the above purpose.
2. Specialists Duties
In order to fulfill the above objectives, the speialist will
assist in:
(a)   developing lending criteria, including their terms and conditions
and set up lending procedures;
(b)   drawing up of farm models for lending purposes;
(c)   building a corps of loan officers and help enhancing appraisal
standards;
(d)   instituting standard procedures for portfolio supervision and
maintaining adequate repayment/collection performance; and
(e)   setting up a program of training of field technicians from DPI
or other government departments and PNGDB with special emphasis
on strengthening their capability in: (i) supporting the above
aspects and, (ii) identification of economically and financially
viable smallholder agricultural projects.
3. Qualifications
The specialist should:
(a)   be proficient in the English language;
(b)   have an academic background (at least a Bachelor's Degree) in
agricultural economics or economics with a banking background;
(c)   have experience in administering agricultural credit programs
in a developing country; and
(d)   be familiar with financial and economic analysis of projects.



ANNEX 2
Appendix 4
Page 1
PAPUA NEW GUINEA
POPONDETTA SMALLHOLDER OIL PALM DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Papua New Guinea Development Bank
Terms of Reference for Consultants
(Systems & Procedures)
1. Introduction:
The Government of Papua New Guinea requires a systems and procedures
specialist to advise on the introduction of simplified work methods in the
Department of Primary Industry (DPI) and the PNG Development Bank (PNGDB).
The position will be for one year with a possible extention for a year and
will be financed out of savings on IDA Credit 175 until June 30, 1976 and
thereafter from savings in Credit 348. The specialist will be employed by the
Government of PNG.
2. Specific Duties:
(a)   The design and introduction of simplified work procedures
for DPI and PNGDB officers covering agricultural develop-
ment programs and associated agricultural credit delivery
systems to smallholders with the emphasis on cost effective-
ness;
(b)   The design and introduction of simplified clerical work
procedures within the PNGDB covering the disbursement,
recording and recovery of agricultural credit;
(c)   To achieve this it will be necessary to:
(i) investigate and recommend improvements in the lines of
communication between DPI and PNGDB;
(ii) investigate and recommend improvements in the organi-
zation of the PNGDB accounts department and in the
work flow between HQ and branches; and
(iii) introduce a simple but effective overall management
reporting and control system for DPI and PNGDB.
(d)   The design and implementation of accounting and reporting
systems for subsequent project to provide data for manage-
ment and financial control, and for future project evaluations;



ANNEX 2
Appendix 4
Page 2
(e)   Other duties may include advice on management struc-
ture aspects of future projects, and comment to manage-
ment on performance and reporting of commercial businesses
owned and/or controlled by PNGDB;
(f)   The implementation stage will require a substantial training
input and the design of simple manuals. This will involve
the preparation and delivery of introductory and explanatory
sessions for all staff :Levels;
(g)   The recruitment and training of local counterpart(s) capable
of maintaining continuity after the assignment has ended; and
(h)   The work will require field trips to DPI and PNGDB offices.
3. Reporting:
The Systems and Procedures Specialist will report to a Coordinating
Committee comprising the Secretary of Finance, the Director of DPI and Manag-
ing Director of PNGDB. For administrative purposes only the specialist will
report to the Secretary of Finance. Within 3 months of his arrival in PNG,
the specialist will submit a brief inception report which will comment on the
work program ad terms of reference and make recommendations for any changes.
Thereafter, the specialist will submit brief quarterly progress reports and a
final report.

PAPUA NEW GUINEA
POPONDETTA SMALLHOLDER OIL PALM DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Oil Palm Development and Research
Hoskins Oil Palm Project
1.        Oil palm was introduced' into PNG in 1967.  The first commercial
planting was established in 1968 in the Hoskins area in the West Nakanai
Division of West New Britain Province by New Britain Palm Oil Development
Comapny Ltd. (NBPOD). The share capital of NBPOD is owned in equal propor-
tions by Government and Harrisons and Crossfield Ltd. (H & C), an overseas
plantation company well experienced in the oil palm industry. NBPOD was
granted a 99 year lease of 4,000 ha of land east of Kimbe, between Talasea
and Hoskins (Map 1) for the purpose of establishing an oil palm plantation
as a nucleus estate.
2.        NBPOD, through three wholly-owned subsidiary companies, owns and
operates the nucleus estate, the palm oil mill and oil tank farm, and the
transport fleet, serving the needs of both nucleus estate and smallholders.
Under agreement with Government, NBPOD provides processing facilities to
smallholder oil palm farmers settled in the surrounding areas. The smallholder
oil palm development in the Hoskins area is being undertaken by Government
with financial assistance from IDA under Credits 137 and 175-PNG. The two
IDA credits were planned to assist some 1,560 settlers and villagers plant
about 5,600 ha of oil palm. To date, about 2,500 ha oil palm has been planted
on the nucleus estate and about 6,050 ha by some 1,750 smallholders.
3.        Each settler has been assisted to establish about 4 ha of oil
palm on his block, together with a food garden and a basic house. The
settlers live on their blocks within walking distance of the community center,
where the basic amenities of an administrative building, primary school,
medical post and small shops are provided. Extension service and welfare
services staff are housed in this center.
4.        Harvesting of FFB on the first plantings started in FY72.  Because
of the unexpectedly high initial FFB yields, the earlier than expected coming
into production of the oil palm sitands and the high world market prices for
palm oil and palm kernels in 1974, smallholders' earnings have been well in
advance of expectations and about 37% of the smallholders have already repaid
their loans.
5.        Since IDA has only been involved in financing the smallholder devel-
opment aspects of the Hoskins project, it has not had influence on the oil mill



ANNEX 3
Page 2
design and on the machinery installed. The oil mill from the outset suf-
fered from insufficient capacity and inadequate machinery. The capacity
problem was contributed to by the project's major success that is that
estate and smallholder yields were much higher and earlier than had been
assumed at the planning stage. These problems have resulted in consider-
ably reduced palm oil extraction and palm kernel recovery rates with con-
sequent reduced returns to the nucleus estate and the smallholders. NBPOD
has taken steps to improve processing efficiency by (i) replacing the
hydraulic presses in the first processing line with more effective screw
presses and (ii) by installing machinery with proven capacity to handle
Tenera FFB in its recently constructed second processing line which in-
creased milling capacity from about 30 to about 60 tons FFB per hour.
6.        The success of the smallholder oil palm development is mainly
due to a group of staff of the Department of Primary Industry (DPI) who
were very personally involved with the project's generation and success-
ful implementation. Services that were not initially provided by special-
ized agencies were provided, as best as was possible, by DPI officers.
A major improvement could have been achieved in the early days if PNGDB
staff had been in the field from the outset.
7.        The project has experienced delays in the settlement program
due to legal problems associated with the reallocation of blocks which had
become vacant or which were never claimed by the successful applicants. Of
the original applicants selected as many as 10% did not take up residence
at their blocks and a further 5% experienced difficulties from the outset
due to infirmities such as old age and therefore unable to implement the
oil palm development satisfactorily. Although legal forfeiture procedures
have improved to allow rapid reallocation of blocks allocated but not
occupied, they are still cumbersome in the case of blocks occupied but
subsequently vacated.
8.        Since there are generally far more applications than available
blocks, the selection of potentially poor-performing settlers could be
avoided by applying improved settler selection criteria. Thus, based on
experience gained in the Hoskins project, other settlement projects and
proven techniques of settler selection in Malaysia, the Department of Lands,
in collaboration with DPI and PNGDB, has drawn up new criteria for settler
selection. These will be used to select settlers for the Popondetta Small-
holder Oil Palm Development Project (paras 21 to 23).
Agronomic Aspects of Oil Palm Cultivation
9.        Experience in the Hoskins project and with trial plots in different
areas have proven that the oil palm grows and yields very well in PNG due to
favorable soil and climatic conditions. Also, unlike in most other countries,
a Pueraria cover crop is easily established. It is noteworthy that this
cover overgrows and eradicates lalang (Imperata Cylindrica - an obnoxious
weed) without any other method of control necessary. Oil palm in PNG grows



ANNEX 3
Page  3
about 100 to 120 cm per annum and produces about 3 leaves per month in its
younger stages; in the later stages it grows about 90 cm per annum and pro-
duces 2 to 2.5 leaves per month.  FFB production starts about 2.5 to 3 years
after planting; all inflorescences which develop up to six months before
commencement of harvesting are castrated. During the first ten years of
production, assisted pollination is required to maintain optimal yields.
Suitable pollen supplies are obtained from all smallholders' blocks. Mixing
of viable pollen with talcum powder is done at the nucleus estate and DASF
personnel distributes the mixture among the smallholders. Because of the
rapid growing characteristics, oil palms may have to be replanted after
26 years, or 23 years of production life.
10.       In order to maintain the high initial yields, fertilizers will
have to be applied as the initial high soil fertility declines. However,
the exact fertilizer requirement under local conditions is not known,
although fertilizer experiments at Dami Research Station indicate that:
(a) increasing muriate of potassium applications result
in highly significant FFB yield increases; however,
with increasing applications, potassium levels in the
fronds decrease while chlorine and calcium levels
increase;
(b) increasing nitrogen applications result in highly
significant growth increments (leaf production, leaf
area and height of the palm) but contrary to findings
in West Africa, Malaysia and Indonesia do not result
in yield increases;
(c) sulphur, either elementary or in the form of sulphate,
may become an important element, since it has a highly
significant effect on coconut yields in PNG; and
(d) trace elements such as magnesium, manganese and boron
may become important in the future.
So far fertilizer know-how gained from Malaysia and Indonesia experience
has been applied in the oil palm plantings in PNG, but it is becoming
clear that on young volcanic soils, with high pH values, other factors have
to be considered. New criteria for nutrient levels in the leaves have to
be established before foliar analysis can become a useful tool for fertilizer
recommendations. It could take about five to ten years of research before
such criteria could be established. The agronomist at Dami has recommended
for the New Brita[n area that in the interim the following fertilizers be
applied:



ANNEX 3
Page 4
(a) 250 grams of sulphate of ammonia per palm, three
months after planting;
(b) no fertilizers be applied during the immature
stage; and
(c) due to harvesting and pruning losses, nutrients
are to be replaced after year 4 based on foliar
analyses.
Phasing of the Project
11.       A time schedule of settlement procedures is presented in Table 1.
In drawing up this schedule due allowance has been made for the process of
advertisements for the blocks, selection of the settlers, transport of the
settler from his home village to the block, land preparation and planting
of oil palm on the block. These procedures are expected to take about one
year. In Chart 2 an implementation schedule is presented of the planting
operations, the settler selection procedures, mill construction and the
infrastructure.
12.       Three types of smallholders would participate in this project:
the existing settlers who would replant their ravaged cocoa areas with oil
palms, the new settlers who will plant oil palms in felled forest, and
villagers who live in the project area and are interested in oil palm
cultivation. The existing and new settlers would plant 4 ha each, all in
one year. The villagers, however, would plant 2 ha annually in two suc-
cessive years, because their social obligations in the village would not
enable them to plant 4 ha all in one year.
13.       The new settlers would be grouped in sub-divisions, each com-
prising about 250 families, around the nucleus estate. The Commonwealth
Development Corporation (CDC) would, in joint venture with Government,
establish a 4,000 ha nucleus estate at Popondetta and also an oil mill with
an ultimate capacity of 60 tons FFB per hour (Annex 4). The nucleus estate
and factory companies would provide the following services to the smallholders:
(a) supply suitable and adequate planting material;
(b) purchase and process smallholders' FFB, and market
all oil and kernel produced;
(c) service the transport fleet of the smallholder FFB
transport company; and
(d) provide training facilities and agricultural technical
advisory services.



ANNEX 3
Page  5
Planting
14.       In the Hoskins project, initially plantings were spaced 150 palms
to the ha, planted in a triangle. HIowever, palm growth in these plantings
was so prolific, that the stand was decreased to 120 palms per ha, planted
in a triange for all plantings after January 1973. This decrease in the
stand is not expected to adversely affect yields. Since palm growth in the
Popondetta trial plots is comparable to palm growth in the Hoskins project,
a stand of 120 palms per ha will be maintained, or 480 palms per 4 ha small-
holder block.  Holdings of this size, supplemented by subsistence food gardens,
have shown to provide the incentive to attract the required family labor
inputs as presented in Table 2. It is expected that family incomes resulting
from the project would increase from the estimated subsistence level of K 516
per annum to an income of about K 1,500 per annum. The planting program for
the nucleus estate and the smallholdLers is presented in Table 3.
FFB Yields
15.       In the project area the rainfall varies from 2,500 to 3,500 mm per
annum, two-thirds of which falls from November to April. Although the period
June to August is relatively dry, Popondetta still receives an average rainfall
of 100 mm a month during this spell. The relative humidity is high, averaging
over 80% throughout the year. Average daily sunshine varies from 5 hours in
October to 6 hours in August, totalling about 2,100 sunshine hours over the
year. The minimum and maximum temperatures vary between 21.5oC and 31.loC,
with an annual mean of 26.50C. Soils are of volcanic origin and free draining.
The pH values range from 6.3 to 6.9 (average 6.5) and are higher than those
in oil palm plantings in Malaysia and Indonesia. The soils in the project
area generally have a light texture and vary from loamy sands to sandy clays.
Their nutrient status is satisfactory, although fertilizers would be required
to maintain high yields. Soils and climatic conditions are optimal to ideal
for oil palm growing.
16.       FFB yield estimates for the nucleus estate and smallholders are
presented in Table 4. These estimates are based on CDC studies and on the
performance of the two oil palm trial plots in Saiho and the Popondetta
Agricultural Training Institute (PATI). Smallholder yields are estimated
at about 80% of the nucleum estate's performance; the latter compares
favorably with yields obtained from estates in Malaysia and in Indonesia.
FFB, oil and kernel production estimates of the nucleus estate and the
smallholders are presented in Table 5. CDC has proposed to erect a 60 ton
FFB per hour oil mill. In Table 6 a review is presented of required mill
capacities, assuming two different levels of peak production. At the proj-
ect's maximum production level (FY88) an oil mill with a 60 ton FFB pet hour
capacity, working 500 hours per month (peak period), would be satisfactory.



ANNEX 3
Page 6
Smallholder Oil Palm Development Costs
17.       PNGDB would provide loans to settlers and villagers participating
in the project. These loans which would cover the on-farm and the settlement
costs would be different for existing settlers, new settlers and villagers.
18.       The on-farm oil palm development costs would include:  a house,
land preparation, harvest and inspection paths, cover crop seed, oil palm
seedlings, tools and equipment, fertilizers, and pest and disease control.
These costs vary for the three types of smallholders as described in the
following notes:
(a) House: all smallholders would be provided a house on
their oil palm block. A simple skillion roofed wooden
building, about 6 x 3 m, with a water tank (about 2.3 m3),
would cost K 700. These houses would be pre-fabricated
and the smallholders, with the assistance of a carpenter,
would build their own houses: the new settler in the
year of oil palm establishment and the existing settlers
and villagers the year after;
(b) Land Preparation: prior to the arrival of the new settlers,
Government contractors would fell the trees on 4.5 ha on
each block - 4 ha for oil palm planting and 0.5 ha for the
house and food gardens. The costs of contract felling are
estimated at K 140 per holding. The settlers would themselves
complete the land clearing operations. Existing settlers and
villagers would receive a loan of K 40, as an incentive to
undertake land clearing on their own;
(c)  Harvest and Inspection Paths:  each smallholder would establish
paths between every other row in his planting. The cost for
this operation is estimated at K 20 per holding;
(d) Cover Crop: the cost of establishig a legume cover is
estimated at K 10 per holding. Most Pueraria seeed would
be imported from Malaysia or Indonesia because the evenly
distributed rainfall in PNG throughout the year hampers
seed production locally. Quarantine regulations stipulate
that imported seed has to be examined in Brisbane's Quarantine
Station before release into PNG;
(e) Oil Palm Seedlings: the nucleus estate would raise the oil
palm seedlings in poly-bags. Dami Research Station has been
providing high-yielding planting material since 1975. The
cost of a seedling (including transport to the smallholder's
block) is estimated at K 1.20 or K 576 per 4 ha holding;



ANNEX 3
Page 7
(f) Tools and Equipment: all srnallholders would be provided
with a loan for the purchase of necessary tools and equip-
ment such as axes, spades, fEiles, bush knife, wheel barrow,
pollen bottles and harvesting chisels in the third year
after planting, estimated at K 138; however, new settlers
would also be provided with a loan for the purchase of
household goods in the first year, estimated at K 110.
Replacement of the tools is estimated at K 15 per annum;
(g)  Fertilizers:  since no definite fertilizer recommendations
can be made, the provision of fertilizer costs are based
on experience in the Hoskins project and on Dami Research
Station findings. Experience in Malaysia and Indonesia
has also been taken into account, although conditions are
different from PNG. However, following experience in
coconut cultivation in the Popondetta area, provision has
been made to include sulphur in the fertilizer program to
avoid possible deficiencies. The following tentative
fertilizer schedule has been used for cost calculations:
-    at planting                    - 100 grams Industrial
Sulphur per palm;
-    3 months after                 - 250 grams Sulphate of
Ammonia per palm;
-    after commencement of           - 1.5 kg Muriate of Potash
harvesting                       and 1 kg Kieserite per
palm per annum;
-    10 years after planting         - 0.75 kg Sulphate of
Ammonia per palm per
annum has been added
to the above schedule;
and
-   no fertilizer applicat-ions during the three years prior
to replanting. Fertilizer recommendations and the costs
for smallholders' plantings are presented in Table 7;
and
(h)  Pest and Disease Control:  so far the common oil palms pests
and diseases have not occurred in PNG. However, with vast
areas under oil palm, pests and diseases incidence should be
anticipated.  Provision is made for pest and disease control
at an estimated annual cost of K 20 per holding.  To ensure
that pests and diseasess arie controlled effectively, this
service would be provided by the project field management
and charged to the smallholders.



ANNEX 3
Page 8
19.       The costs of settling a smallholder's family would include:
removal expenses, subsistence and living allowances, lease preparation,
land rent, land survey fee, and loan and legal fees. PNGDB would provide
loans to the smallholders to cover the costs involved. These costs also
vary for the three types of smallholders as described in the following
notes:
(a) Removal Expenses: the average costs of moving a new
settler and his family from his home village to the
project area are estimated at K 150;
(b) Subsistance Allowance: the new settler requires money
to buy food until his food gardens start producing.
Immediately after arrival, the settler would start
planting his food garden, using planting material
provided by the extension service staff. About five
months after planting, the garden should provide
sufficient food for the settler and his family. Thus,
the subsistance allowance provided would cover a period
of five months at K 43 a month and would amount to
K 215 per settler;
(c) Living Allowance: for new settlers, who may have insuf-
ficient income until their oil palm plantings start pro-
ducing, a living allowance of K 10 per month would, during
the first three years, be provided to meet their needs
other than food;
(d) Lease Preparation: the initial lease preparation fee is
K 10 per new settler;
(e) Land Rent: all new and existing settlers would pay an
annual rent of K 30 per holding; and
(f) Land Survey Fee: new settlers would pay a K 150 fee for
having his block surveyed and for having cement markers
placed at the block corners; and the fee to be paid in
the year after planting.
20.       Table 8 summarizes oil palm development costs; farm models for the
new settler, the existing settler and the villager are presented in Tables 9
to 11 and a summary for the smallholder oil palm project development costs is
presented in Table 12.
Settler Selection
21.       Selection of applicants for settlement on an oil palm block will be
done by the Land Board, a statutory body of the Department of Lands, under pro-
vision of the Land Act. Settler selection would be on a nationwide basis, but
preference will be given to applicants from the Northern Province.



ANNEX 3
Page 9
22.       Generally, there are far more applications for settlement than there
are blocks available and it would be possible to select settlers who would
meet the following criteria:
(a) Married (monogamous) with 2 to 4 children;
(b) Ideally about 30 years old;
(c) Healthy and physically fit;
(d) Having a satisfactory educational standard;
(e) Being able to communicate either in English or
in pidgin;
(f) Satisfactory character references;
(g) Having experience in tree crops cultivation;
(h)  Being landless or having land unsuitable for
cash crops; and
(i) Having vocational experience.
23.       Points would be awarded for the above criteria and the Land Board
would select future settlers among those applicants scoring the highest number
of points. A points system has been successfully used in a rubber settlement
scheme and this system will be considered for settler selection for the
Popondetta smallholder oil palm development project.
Oil Palm Research
24.       Currently, oil palm research is concentrated in the Dami Research
Station (Hoskins project area). This Research Station is owned and operated
by NBPOD. One agronomist is in charge of all field trials and experiments.
Research is mainly directed at progeny testing of high-yielding material
ex-Malaysia. Harrisons Flemming Advisory Services with assistance from an
independent qualified geneticist have selected promising parent palms (Dura
and Pisifera), which would provide high-yielding Tenera planting material.
In the progeny trials some "dumpy" material from Malaysia is included, but
serious research on developing high-yielding dwarfs has not yet started.
Other research activities are restricted to spacing and fertil-4zer trials.
25.       Dami Research Station takes care of the research needs of the
Hoskins project on New Britain. Because the Popondetta area has different
soil and climatic characteristics, oil palm research needs of thin area have
to be specially taken care of. The two oil palm observation plots at Saiho
(sandy clay loam derived from volcanic deposits, annual rainfall 3,400 mm)
and at PATI (loamy sand, annual rainfall 2,400 mm) show striking differences



ANNEX 3
Page 10
in growth and production. These differences are mainly due to the different
soil and climatic conditions and partly to differences in maintenance standards
in the past.
26.       A research program for the Popondetta area should focus on fertilizer
research on the loam and sandy soils to establish optimal nutrient levels in
the fronds, upon which foliar analysis could be used for making firm recommen-
dations for fertilizer applications appropriate for these soil types. These
fertilizer experiments could be combined with spacing experiments in order to
establish an optimal stand per ha.
27.       Smallholders would also benefit from the outcome of castration and
intercropping experiments. The former would establish the optimal time to
which FFB production should be postponed to avoid over-bearing of the palms
which could result in fluctuating and even decreasing yields. The latter would
establish the agricultural standards and the economics of intercropping food
crops between the oil palms during the first eighteen months after planting.
28.       Progeny testing of high yielding ex-Malaysia material and of dwarf
hybrids (E. Oleifera x E. Guineensis) will be left to Dami Research Station.
Oil palm breeding is a long-term research program and results could be applied
when the present plantings are due for replanting.
29.       PNG Government intends to have research as described in para 26,
carried out at the nucleus estate with the possibility that the scientific
input of the program be provided by Dami Research Station and the day to
day management of the experiments be provided by the nucleus estate company.
Experimental results could be interpreted by Dami Research Station staff
and be made available to the PNG oil palm industry.

ANNEX 4
Page 1
PAPUA NEW GUINEA
POPONDETTA SMALLHOLDER OIL PALM DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Nucleus Estate and Processing Factory
1.         As part of its oil palm development program for the Popondetta
area, Government will conclude an agreement with the Commonwealth Development
Corporation (CDC) according to which CDC will establish and operate a nucleus
estate and a palm oil mill. To complement the smallholder project, tentative
agreement has been reached that provides for the establishment of an Estate
Company (EC) and a Factory Company (FC), to be jointly owned by Government
and CDC.
2.        The CDC participation in the proposed FC and EC joint venture was
initially appraised by a mission from London in May 1974. A subsequent up-
dating mission took place in May 1975 and a basis for agreement was finally
reached between Government and CDC at the time of the Bank appraisal of the
smallholder component in February 1976. Final documents will be signed upon
confirmation that the smallholder development will take place. At the same
time and as a condition of effectiveness of the proposed Bank project, it
will be required that agreements between Government and CDC covering the
establishment, financing and operation of the two companies will have been
concluded and made available to the Bank.
Proposed Development
3.        It is proposed that the EC, at full development, will consist of
4,000 ha planted with oil palm. Commencing in FY78, the planting program
will extend over a 5 year period as detailed below and shown in relation to
the proposed smallholder project:
FY78    FY79    FY80    FY81   FY82   Total
Estate (ha)           400      600    1,000   1,000  1,000  4,000
Smallholder (ha)      300   1,100    1,772   1,648    780  5,600 4.
Land allocation and acquisition for the FC and EC, involving areas
to the East and West of the Amboga River (see Map), has been arranged by the
National Investment Development Authority (NIDA) in conjunction with the
Lands Department.
5.        It is proposed that the FC will ultimately provide oil palm milling
capacity for 60 tons FFB/hour, comprising two lines of 30 tons/hour each.
Proposed mill size takes account of assumed yield profiles and estimated
production figures, taking monthly peak yields as being as high as 14% of
the annual totals (Annex 3, Tables 4, 5, and 6). Associated developments



ANNEX 4
Page 2
will include the construction of central workshops, a bulk oil installation
and storage at Oro Bay.
6.        Mill design has been discussed with the Bank and influenced by ex-
perience gained from the Hoskins project. It is expected that many of the
start-up problems that occcurred there will be avoided. The proposed con-
struction schedule takes account of the expected rate of smallholder and
estate development and according to that, the mill should be operative by
mid 1980.
Estimated Costs and Financing
7.        Allowing for expected price increases and physical contingencies,
development costs are estimated to be K 13.52 M (US$17.17 M) for the FC and K
8.19 M (US$10.40 M) for the EC, making a total of K 21.71 M (US$27.57 M).
These costs are detailed in Table 1. It has been tentatively proposed that
equity funds amounting to K 7.50 M (US$9.52 M) be subscribed by Government and
CDC in equal amounts, with the balance of the development needs met by debt
financing. Table 2 shows details of proposed FC and EC financing. Subject to
their Boards confirmation it has been indicated that CDC would contribute in
total about K 8.66 M (US$11 M) towards the overall requirements for debt and
equity.
Operation and Management
8.        In the first years of development, the EC will have resposibility for
nursery operations and the supply to smallholders of all planting material.
Subsequently, the FC will be responsible for the pricing, purchasing and pro-
cessing of all smallholder (and EC) FFB and the final marketing of the palm
oil and kernel production. Although smallholder FFB collection and transporta-
tion will be the responsibility of a separate company (Annex 8), full vehicle
servicing facilities will be provided by the FC.
9.        It is proposed that the provision of a full corporate management team
will be the responsibility of CDC. Administration, agricultural and engineer-
ing personnel required for the establishment and development of FC and EC will
be provided or specifically recruited by CDC. It is intended that CDC provide
the Chief Executive and Company Secretary under agreements with EC and FC.
(Both companies would share the same officers.) Additionally the CDC manage-
ment agreements will provide for a fixed number of Visiting Agents visits per
annum for special agricultural and engineering aspects.
10.       The EC will service the sma]lholder sector by purchasing planting
material (germinated seed), growing the material in nurseries and selling the
material to smallholders for planting. The EC management would also provide
technical services in the form of agriicultural advice and arranging the
bulk purchasing of farm materials such as agro-chemicals and equipment. A
liaison officer attached to the EC would ensure coordination of harvesting and
fruit deliveries to the factory from both the EC and smallholders.



ANNEX 4
Page 3
11.       FFB production by the EC will be sold by weight to the factory,
delivered over the factory weigh bridge. On the basis of various FFB price
assumptions and updated cost estimates, CDC have determined the rate of
return on the EC investment to be satisfactory. In relation to the FC, it is
proposed that all operations would be conducted on a fully commercial basis,
rather than as a service to smallholders and the EC. It is proposed that
the factory will purchase all fruit as principal at a price that will permit
a satisfactory margin over the costs of processing and marketing.

PAPUA NEW GUINEA
POPONDETTA SMALLHOLDER OIL PALM DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Road Requirements, Infrastructure,
Staff and Transportation
General
1.        The project area is located on the coastal plain adjacent to the
town of Popondetta and about 25 km north of Mount Lamington, an active
volcano, which controls the watershed and consequently the drainage pattern
in the project area. The Kumusi River 20 km to the west of Popondetta
rises in the hinterland and drains northwards to the sea. The influence
of Mount Lamington has created a radial pattern of deeply incised drainage
channels which in the project area run generally northwards. The township
of Popondetta is approximately 30 km from the coast in the east and 20 km
in the north. The area was used as military base during World War II and
a track access across the Owen Stanley Ranges was then established between
Port Moresby and Kokoda and then Popondetta. Since that time a good two
lane gravel road from Kokoda extends 110 km through Popondetta to the coastal
port at Oro Bay. The rugged Owen Stanley Ranges have not as yet been crossed
with a road so that the only link the area now has with the rest of Papua
New Guinea is by sea or air.
Township
2.        The township of Popondetta grew up with the soldier settlements
which were developed for cocoa production. The town now has all the basic
facilities necessary for its present population of approximately 1,500.
Popondetta is the headquarters for the administration of the Northern
Province and is served by government's service departments. There is a
modern 30 bedroom hotel, two established recreational clubs, trade stores,
bank, and sub-branches of the major distributors that are located in Lae
or Port Moresby. The town is properly laid out with bitumin surfaced roads
including curb and guttering. The water supply is pumped from the river,
treated and reticulated throughout the town. The power supply is from a
diesel generator and provides 24 hour service. The educational and hospital
facilities are adequate. Popondetta is the only established township in
the area of influence and it would be the only logistic center available
to the project.
Existing Road Network
3.        The Popondetta area has been developed in isolation from the rest
of the country.  There is only one first class road which leads generally
westwards from the Port at Oro Bay 50 km to Popondetta and continues a
further 60 km to the foothills of the Owen Stanley Ranges terminating at



ANNEX .)
Page 2
the township of Kokoda. This road is 8 m formation generally paved with
a mixture of pumice and untreated river gravel. The road would have a
capacity without upgrading of about 600 vehicles per day in its present
condition. Certain sections are more heavily trafficked especially between
Popondetta and the Girua Airport and this section is currently being
upgraded with a double bituminous surface treatment.
4.        From this main road lead a large number of minor feeder roads and
access tracks. These vary from 3 m wide roads with river gravel surface to
3 m wide earth roads. The alignment of many of these roads is suitable for
project development traffic but the formation requires widening and
resurfacing.
5.        The bridging on the main road between the oil palm mill site and
the port is to HS 20 1/ highway standards. The bridges are generally steel
girder with composite concrete decks but there are two bridges of pre-stressed
concrete design. The bridge design standard is equal to the country's high-
way standard for bridges and is capable of carrying the road tankers or heavy
trucks that are likely to be used in the project.
6.        The terrain in the area of influence is flat with deep gullies
cut into the sandy loam of the volcanic deposits. River gravels are generally
abundant but these decrease with the distance from Mount Lamington. With
the drainage pattern generally north-south and the main access east-west
the existing feeder roads are all running north-south between the drainage
channels.
Airports
7.        A number of airfields were constructed in the area during World
War II and one of these has been reconstructed to form the airport at
Girua 14 km east of Popondetta. This airport is served by F27 Fokker
Friendship aircraft with a daily service to Port Moresby.
Seaports
8.        A new steel piled concrete deck wharf has been constructed at
Oro Bay. This wharf is identical in size to the wharf at Kimbe that serves
the Hoskins oil palm project. The shore installations have storage
facilities sufficient only for the present needs. Significant improvements
would be required to the layout of the shore installation to meet the
requirements of the tank farm. It is also necessary to improve the
foreshore protection works.
1/   NAASRA (National Association of Australian State Road Authorities) HS20
loading is identical to HS20-S16 used by AASHO in North America. This
allows a G.V.W. of 36 tons and maximum axle loading of 9 tons and 16 tons
respectively for single and tandem axles.



ANNEX 5
Page 3
Communication
9.        Popondetta is connected to the Papua New Guinea automatic telephone
system by radio links. This provides a good service in PNG and to world
networks.
Road Requirements
General
10.       The nucleus estate is well served with existing roads and it
requires only a minimum of work to bring these roads into service. However,
the roads in the nucleus estate have not been included in the project. In
the eastern sector there is no existing access to the mill site except by
a long detour through the township of Popondetta. To avoid this, a bridge
and road have been included in the estimates to permit an access road to
lead directly to the mill site from the eastern sector. Similarly in the
western sector, allowance has been made to construct new roads and bridges
that permit direct access to the miLl site. Each of the areas are discussed
with a summary of roads required.
Existing Settlements
11.       Immediately surrounding the nucleus estate are the existing settle-
ments. The roads in these areas are sometimes overgrown and poorly drained;
all requiring upgrading with additional width necessary on access roads. Road
requirements are summarized below:

12.       The costs for West Amnboga include access roads which serve
adjacent areas and are consequently high per block. North Sangara has a
long access for a small number of blocks. These could well be rearranged
in the final design as it is one of the highest unit costs in the prelimi-
nary layout. Sangara has a low unit cost per block because a large pro-
portion of the blocks are located on the existing main road.  East Amboga
costs are high because of the Gawana Bridge which is included in this
section.
New Settlements
13.       The areas for new settlers are adjacent to the existing settle-
ment areas. In the eastern sector the new settler areas do not have direct
access to the oiL palm factory as there is no crossing of the Gawana River.
Provision has been made for an access road which connects Soputa, Serovi
and the eastern portion of the nucleus estate with the main road by a bridge
of HS20 standard over the Gawana River at a point almost due east of the
oil palm mill.  These costs have been included in the road costs for exist-
ing settlers.  Egora and Isivene are connected to the existing system witl
sub-divisional roads, some of which reach access road standard.  There is
an existing road to Wijo but upgrading is required.  Road requirements are

Road Standards
16.       It is intended that smallholders' FFB be hauled to the oil palm
mill in 10 ton two axle trucks. These trucks generally carry about 6.8
tons of fruit. The road standard proposed is a 6 meter formation with
4 meters of 15 cm selected river gravel. Some of the sub-divisional roads
are reduced in width to 5 meter formation with 3 meter of 15 cm selected
river gravel. At intervals along the road platforms are intended for the
loading of fruit and here the road is widened to permit the passage of other
traffic. It is expected that each fifty blocks would generate about 8
vehicles per day so that the access roads which generally link 200 blocks
would require to provide for 50 vehicles per day at full production. The
road standards have been set with this in mind. It is possible in some cases
for traffic outside the project to use the roads. This is of particular
importance in the link Togahau - Agenahambo which if the Kumusi timber
project proceeds would be a likely route for timber extraction. If this
were the case considerable upgrading of this link would be imperative.
Road Layout
17.       The blocks are generally 6 ha in area with dimensions of approxi-
mately 200 x 300 meters. The sub-divisional roads have been estimated
based upon each block having a road frontage of approximately 200 meters.
Therefore, to provide access on the ideal layout for 1,400 blocks would be
140 km of road. The actual layout causes an increase of about 50% over
140 km or 220 km giving an overall average of 157 meters of road per block.
The average cost per block for road works is K 1,548.

18.       The estimates for roadworks are based on costs for similar works
in both Popondetta and Kimbe. This resulted in the following unit costs:

kWad Construction
20.       The road construction consists of clearing a formation in sandy
loam material 8 meters wide for access roads with side drains 30 cm deep.
As the clearing is done with bulldozers leaving no grubbing to be done the
earthworks for the roads can be accomplished with graders. The sandy loam
is easily compacted resulting in a simple operation. Culverts in the area
are corrugated steel nestable flange type which are easily transported and
placed. Pavement materials are available from river gravel deposits gene-
rally on a 5 km haul. Processing is not required other than to hand pick
out the larger gravel sizes and to select those parts of the river deposits
that have a natural grading suitable for pavements.
21.       This type of construction makes it possible to undertake the work
by contract without the need for very detailed specifications. There are
two well established contractors based in Popondetta together with a number
of small trucking firms. These resources could easily be mobilized to under-
take most of the work including the bridging where upgrading is required.
22.       The variable nature of the work could make the definition of the
work difficult for contract work and may best be undertaken by the Public
Works road construction forces in Popondetta. The proportions of each type
of construction are at mid-1976 prices (including physical contingencies):
New Roads                  K 1,280,000
Upgrading                  K   647,000
Bridges                    K   240,000
Total       K 2,167,000
It is assumed that design and supervision of these works which are scheduled
over a 4 year period would be done by the Department of Public Works. Details
of road costs and construction cash flows are given in Table 1.



ANNEX 5

Infrastructure
23.       New settlers participating in the project would come from different
parts of PNG, drawn from heterogeneous backgrounds. Substantial social in-
frastructure would be necessary to make the settlement process a smooth one.
The facilities to be provided include community centers, schools, medical
aid posts, markets, trade store sites and police posts and for housing of
extension staff.
24.       Community Development Officers would actively assist in reorienting
settler attitudes from one of subsistence economy to the new cash economy.
They will also help the settlers in adjusting to the new life style. Coopera-
tive Development Officers would help in reducing cultural antagonisms between
ethnic groups. Social and extension infrastructure costs are shown in Tables
2 and 3.
Staff Transportation
25.       Government has recently established a Plant and Transport Authority
(PTA) which is responsible for purchase and maintenance of all vehicles,
machinery and equipment used by all Government Departments. The Departments
pay for the hire of these and are now not permitted to own and maintain their
own vehicles, machinery and equipment. The PTA has facilities located strate-
gically throughout the country to service the needs of the various departments.
Its hire charges, although high are nonetheless realistic since they reflect
the high maintenance costs and rapid depreciation under the rugged PNG condi-
tions. Vehicle machinery and equipment requirements and costs for the Project
are shown in Table 4.

Technical Services and Staffing
Terms of Reference
I. Project Management Committee
Project Accountant
1.        The Project Accountant would be responsible to and report directly
to the Project Coordinator. He would be a qualified member of a recognized
Chartered Accountants or Certified Public Accountants Institute with a minimum
of five years experience in financial and cost accounting and auditing, gained
preferably from agro-industrial operations. His responsibilities would
include:
(a)  advising the Project Coordinator on all financial and accounting
matters connected with the project;
(b)  setting up financial, statistical and accounting procedures
and records necessary for project implementation;
(b)  devising and preparing cost accounts for all project operations;
(d)  preparation of annual PMC budgets, and budgetary control of pro-
ject costs;
(e)  project expenditure payments and bank accounts;
(f)  checking statements of accounts and bills received from, and
submitted to government departments, nucleus estate companies
and other agencies connected with the project;
(g)  preparation of reimbursement/withdrawal applications for submis-
sion to the Bank and PNGDB;
(h)  preparation of composite project costs (monthly and quarterly),
and annual accounts in a form suitable for verification and
reporting by independent auditors;
(i)  assisting the Project Manager in revising and reviewing
costs of smallholder farm models and processing and marketing
costs charged by nucleus estate companies to smallholders;



ANNEX 6
Appendix 1
Page 2
(j) helping the Project Manager in preparing quarterly progress
reports for submission to Project Management Committee and
the Bank.
II. Transport Company (TC)
The Manager
2.        The Manager would be the chief executive of the TC.  He would be
a qualified engineer or a member of a recognized Institute of Transport and
be experienced in the management of transport fleets. His responsibilities
would include:
(a) maintaining close contact with the Project Management Committee,
Project Manager Field (PMF), Factory Company (FC) and assist in
setting up the TC;
(b) assist with the setting up of the TC office, procurement of
vehicles, and recruitment of staff for the TC;
(c) liaising with PM and FC and arrange for efficient transport
operations, and maintenance of transport fleet;
(d) reporting to TC Board on Cornpany budgets and actual results
and performance;
(e) fixing, reviewing and revising transport charges to smallholders;
and
(f) liaising with FC and PNGDB and making payment of FFB dues to
smallholders after deducting loan repayments due to PNGDB.
Accountant/Financial Controller
3.        The Accountant would be responsible to and report directly to the
TC Manager. He would be a qualified member of a recognized Chartered Account-
ants or Certified Public Accountants' Institute with minimum of five years'
experience, preferable in an industrial or commercial organization. His re-
sponsibilities would include:
(a) advising the TC Manager on all financial and accounting matters
connected with the project and assisting him with preparation
of quarterly and other reports;
(b) setting up of a proper system of accounting, costing, budgetary
control and management reporting for the company; and



ANNEX 6
Appendix 1
Page 3
(c) arranging for annual audits of the company's accounts.
Field Superintendent
4.        The Field Superintendent would assist the TC Manager in the physical
aspects of the company's operations and be experienced in smallholder agricul-
tural projects. His responsiblities would inlcude:
(a) organizing harvesting rosters;
(b) arranging FFB collection schedules;
(c) supervising the above operations and coordinating transport re-
quirements;
(d) maintaining and distributing nets to smallholders for FFB
collection.

PAPUA NEW GUINEA
POPONDETTA SMALLHOLDER OIL PALM DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Smallholder FFB Marketing
1.        The effective marketing of smallholder FFB, involving primarily
the transportation and pricing of fruit, is of fundamental importance to
the success of the proposed project and is of equal ranking to those aspects
associated with oil palm cultivation and FFB production. In fact, experience
from the Hoskins project has shown that problems associated with smallholder
FFB marketing are more likely to occur than are production problems, given
the fertility of the coastal areas and the competence of extension staff.
A. Transportation
2.        In the Hoskins project, all aspects of smallholder FFB transporta-
tion involving FFB collection and weighing, FFB delivery to the factory, net
distribution and net maintenance has been the responsibility of the Factory
Company (FC). These activities were initially assigned to the Mosa mill as a
temporary expedient but have remained with that company ever since for want
of the formation of a more appropriate executing agency.
3.        The point at issue on the question of smallholder FFB transporta-
tion derives from the fact that Government is ultimately responsible for
the achievement of smallholder development. In the past when problems have
arisen in connection with FFB prices and/or collection rosters, it has proven
unsatisfactory and unfortunate for the factory company to be too closely
identified with the Government role. In fact at Hoskins, good relations
between smallholders and the factory company have actually suffered as a
result of the company being seen to operate as the purchaser of the individual
smallholder's fruit, the authority for rejecting unsatisfactory fruit and
as the organization responsible for obtaining the 'best price' possible.
For the proposed development program at Popondetta, the achievement of a
highly efficient mill operation will be possible, although difficult and
the task will only be complicated if the Hoskins model is followed and the
FC made responsible for smallholder FFB transportation. Accordingly, it
is proposed to establish a separate Government owned Transport Company (TC)
under the project.



ANNEX 7
Page 2
Transport Company (TC): Objectives
4.        (a)  To operate a vehicle fleet for FFB collection
and delivery to the FC;
(b) To organize harvesting rosters and accompanying
FFB collection schedules;
(c) To provide, maintain and distribute nets for FFB
collection;
(d)  To act as the authority for rejecting unsatisfactory
fruit; and
(e)  To effect FFB payments to smallholders, net of loan
repayments and transportation costs.
Ownership and Operation
5.        The company would be Government owned, either directly or through
an agency, with provision for an ultimate transfer of ownership to small-
holder interests. The company board would consist of representatives of
DPI, Ministry of Finance, PNGDB, the District Commissioner and the Factory
Company. Management of the TC would be undertaken by CDC according to a
fixed term contract.  CDC would recruit key personnel namely, the TC Manager,
Accountant and Field Superintendent. Reflecting the proposed smallholder
planting program and the period cf plant immaturity, the TC would not com-
mence operations until mid-1980. Assurances have been obtained during
negotiations that Government would incorporate the company, arrange manage-
ment contracts, recruit personnel in line with agreed terms of reference,
and subscribe the necessary debt and equity capital.
6.        The TC would perform as a separate operating entity from the FC
but close cooperation between the two companies would be assured with the
appointment of a Smallholder Liaison Officer within the FC and the common
involvement of CDC in the management of both companies. The TC would also
maintain close contact with the smallholder project's field management.
7.        To avoid the duplication of vehicle maintenance, equipment and
spare parts inventory, the servicing of TC vehicles would be performed on
a contract basis by the FC.
8.        Transport services would be charged to smallholders at a rate of
K 5.50 per ton FFB. This corresponds to the figure being charged in the
Hoskins project and would generate sufficient revenue to cover all costs.



ANNEX 7
Page 3
Capital Requirements and Financing
9.        Fixed capital requirements of the TC, involving the construction
of an office building and staff housing, together with the purchase of trans-
port vehicles and associated equipment are estimated to be K 475,000 up
to the end of FY84 (see Tables I and 3). Allowing for expected price increases,
the figure is estimated to be K 817,000. Staff requirements and costs are
presented in Table 2. The foreign exchange component of capital requirements
over the proposed disbursement period is estimated at 75%, and this would be
eligible for the Bank financing.
B. Pricing
10.       The basis for FFB pricing for the Hoskins project is the subject
of an agreement between Government and the New Britain Palm Oil Development
Company (NBPOD). According to this agreement, each month the Government
gazettes a local FOB price equivalent to the CIF price in Europe less agreed
charges for:
(a) freight;
(b) insurance;
(c) commission;
(d) wastage;
(e) shipping losses; and
(f) overseas wharf handling.
11.       After taking into account export duty of 2-1/2% FOB 1/, an ex-mill
value of palm oil is determined by also deducting agreed charges for:
(a) local wharf handling;
(b) NBPOB oil pumping and storage facilities;
(c) cost of financing bulk storage;
(d) transportation cost of oil and kennels from
factory to wharf; and
(e) the factory profit margin (5% of CIF).
1/   Suspended since mid-1975 because of low world prices for palm oil.



ANNEX 7
Page 4
12.       By using a 13 period average mill extraction rate, the ex-mill
value per ton of FFB is determined from which a deduction for milling
charges and cartage yields the monthly FFB farm gate price for smallholders.
Table 4 sets out the detail of these calculations according to which an
FFB price of K 22.50 per ton has been derived from a CIF price of US$400
per ton of oil and $250 per ton of kernel.
13.       The Hoskins FFB price formula is based upon the underlying premise
that the mill company must, over the medium-term, cover costs and earn a
reasonable profit if the project -is to successfully foster oil palm devel-
opment. Furthermore, despite the experience of widely fluctuating palm
oil prices, it is apparent that the revenue that has been left for small-
holders has provided them with adequate incomes and sufficient incentive
for them to maintain production, subject to there being some flexibility
in PNGDB loan repayment requirements in times of very low prices (para 4.26).
14.       The Hoskins price formula has been subject to various changes since
FFB marketing first commenced and all of them have been designed to correct
the initial lack of profitability in the mill.
15.       In the proposed project at Popondetta it is intended to use the same
rationale and basic formula for FFB pricing. However, determination of the
precise cost coefficients would be deferred until FFB marketing commences
and can therefore benefit from the additional experience gained from the
Hoskins project over the next 3 years. Assurances would be obtained during
negotiations that when the time comes to formulate a precise FFB pricing
mechanism, the Bank will be consulted.

PAPUA NEW GUINEA
POPONDETTA SMALLHOLDER OIL PALM DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Project Management
National Projects
1.         The National Executive Council (NEC) has been delegated the author-
ity to declare particular development projects which are of national signifi-
cance and have high government priority to be National Projects. In general,
National Projects will have the following characteristics:
(a)   substantial central government input; (b)
involvement and coordination of different Government
Departments and Agencies;
(c)   development directed in more than one Province or alternatively
a major development in one Province significantly affecting
existing development; and
(d)   Government commitment with either International Credit and/or
Aid Agencies or Foreign Investors or both.
NEC has declared the Popondetta Smallholder Oil Palm Development Project a
National Project. Based on the experience in the Hoskins project Government
proposes to set up a management organization as described below and as
presented in Chart 3.
Project Management Committee (PMC)
2.         For National Projects certain procedures concerning planning,
coordination, organization and management have been created. For each
National Project a Project Management Committee would be established to co-
ordinate the planning, management and implementation of the project. The PMC
for the Popondetta Smallholder Oil Palm Development Project has been recently
constituted as follows:
(a)   The Chairman, being the Secretary of DPI or his nominee,
reporting to the Minister of Primary Industry;
(b)   The Secretary of the Department of Finance or his nominee;
(c)   The Secretary of the Department of Natural Resources or
his nominee;
(d)   The Director of the Department of The National Works Authority
or his nominee;



ANNEX 10
Page 2
(e)   The Technical Director for the project being a senior specialist
officer of the functional department (DPI);
(f)   The Managing Director of PNGDB or his nominee; and
(g)   The Provincial Commissioner of Northern Province.
3.         PMC should be a small working committee, but it is envisaged that
for some National Projects such as for the Popondetta Smallholder Oil Palm
Development Project the following Departments could be represented - if so
needed - in PMC.
(a)   Department of Health;
(b)   Department of Education;
(c)   Department of Police; and
(d)   Department of Labor, Industry and Commerce.
4.         The PMC would have the following responsibilities and authorities:
(a)   PMC would be responsible for the overall iplementation and manage-
ment of the National Project based on NEC approval and Government
agreement with International Agencies and external investors. PMC
through its Chairman will report on all matters pertaining to the
project to the Minister of the Functional Department who in turn
will report to NEC;
(b)   PMC would be responsible for all project expenditures, although
it may delegate this responsibility to the Project Coordinator;
(c)   PMC would be responsible for periodic reports on physical implemen-
tation and financial expenditures of the project; such reports to
be prepared at least every three months; and
(d)   PMC would be responsible that Government obligations with regard
to International Agencies and external investors are fully complied
with and that problems would be immediately referred through the
appropriate channels to the International Agencies and/or external
investors concerned.
Technical Director
5.        The Technical Director (TD) located in DPI would be responsible to
the Secretary DPI for the physical implementation of the oil palm project. He
would exercise direct authority over the Project Manager Field (PM) and he
would liaise between and work closely with specialists and consultants in such
fields as extension, training, plantation management, oil mill technology,
market and transport management.



ANNEX 10
Page 3
6.         The TD would be a Government appointed Director (or Alternate
Director to the Secretary of DPI) on the boards of the nucleus estate, the
factory and the transport companies during the period of project implemen-
tation. He would be responsible for monitoring the performance of those
companies and would report to the Secretary of DPI and the PMC as required.
Project Coordinator
7.         The Project Coordinator (PC) would be the Assistant Secretary
for the recently created Office of Project Coordination (OPC) in the
Department of Finance. He would be the executive officer of the PMC and
assisted by a project accountant he would be responsible for preparing and
monitoring the budgets, the accounting and management reports, and the
reimbursement claims for the project. Apart from administering he would also
exercise financial control over the project funds.
8.         The PC would liaise between and work closely with the line depart-
ments and agencies involved in project implementation and would ensure that
sufficient and adequate staff, services and supplies would be available to
carry out the approved work programs. He would cooperate closely with the
PM on the financial and non-agricultural aspects of the programs and based
on the PM's and other line department's financial reporting would provide
the PM financial statements, budgets and other information on project imple-
mentation progress.
9.         The PC would be a Government appointed Director (or Alternate
Director to the Secretary of Finance) on the boards of the nucleus estate,
the factory and the transport companies during the period of project imple-
mentation.
Project Manager Field
10.       The Project Manager (PM) would be responsible for project implemen-
tation at field level. He would be an officer of the functional line depart-
ment (DPI) or a specially appointed officer. He would be directly responsible
to the TD for all matters concerning project implementation and on all matters
concerning extension staff and technical issues of oil palm development.
11.        The PM would be qualified and experienced in managing agricultural
projects in a senior technical, financial or administrative capacity. His
responsibilities would include:
(a)   implementing the smallholder oil palm development utilizing the
project extension staff;
(b)   preparing and carrying out training programs for field extension
staff and smallholder farmers;



ANNEX 10
Page 4
(c)   ascertaining results from research stations and the r.ucleus estate
company for application in smallholders' plantings;
(d)   arranging for inspection of oil palm nurseries and planting materials
supplied to the smallholders by the nucleus estate company;
(e)   channelling agricultural technical advisory services offered by the
nucleus estate company and implementation of the recommendations
made by visiting inspection service teams;
(f)   reviewing and revising costs of smallholder farm models;
(g)   administering project expenditures within the budgets as
approved by PMC;
(h)   liaising with the nucleus estate and the factory companies, the
smallholder FFB transport company, PNGDB, Government line depart-
ments and other agencies, involved in project operations; and
(i)   submitting periodical and annual accounts, quarterly progress
reports and other information to the PC and through him to the
PMC and the Bank.
Funds
12.        Government funds for a National Project would be appropriated
annually in the National Development Plrojects Division of the Appropriation
Act. This appropriation would provide funding for all Government expendi-
tures in the project except for field staff salaries which would be provided
out of the respective line departments' budgets. The PMC would be responsible
for expenditure of the appropriate funds, however, it could delegate this
responsibility to t -e PC.
13.        The PMC would also be responsible for expenditure of funds pro-
vided by external money lenders (International Agencies such as the Bank
Group, ADB etc.). Thie PC would be responsible for the preparation of dis-
bursement claims.

PAPUA NEW GUINEA
POPONDETTA SMALLHOLDER OIL PALM DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Market Prospects for Palm Oil
1.        After a period of slow growth (1950-65), world production of palm
oil expanded rapidly in recent years (Graph 1). Since only a small portion
of it was consumed in producing countries, increasing quantities began to
flow into the international markets. World exports, which stagnated through-
out the 1950s and early 1960s, increased at a rate of 15% a year after 1967.
In 1975 the share of palm oil in exports of all fats and oils had reached 13%
-- the second largest share after soybean oil.
2.        The Bank's staff projects world production of fats and oils to in-
crease by 88% between 1976 and 1985. During this period, palm oil will ex-
pand its share from 6.8 to 10.1%. Most of this expansion will come from oil-
palms planted during the late sixties which will reach their peak yields
during this period. (New plantings between now and 1980 will not affect palm
oil output significantly before 1985). Oilpalm projects financed by the Bank
Group will contribute about 10.1% to the world production of palm oil by
1985.
3.        Internal consumption of palm oil in developing countries is expected
to grow rapidly during the next decade (1976-85), while palm oil exports
(mainly to developed countries) are projected to grow even faster. Thus the
share of palm oil in the world trade of fats and oils will rise from 11.8%
in 1976 to 22.9% in 1985. Malaysia will continue to dominate exports fol-
lowed by Indonesia: the Ivory Coast and Zaire will be the main exporters
in West Africa (Graph 2).
4.        Developed countries import about 90 percent of palm oil traded in
international markets. Germany, Netherlands and the United Kingdom account
for almost 60% of world palm oil imports (Graph 3). While the shares of
the three European countries declined slightly during the past two decades
(1955-75), the share of US palm oil imports increased by 300% during the
last decade (1965-75). The growing demand for palm oil in the United
States reflects the trends towards greater use of vegetable oils in con-
sumer products and the decline in the price of palm oil relative to that
of other vegetable oils.
5.        Technically, most fats and oils are interchangeable.  The market
outlook for each oil must therefore be viewed within the context of the
overall market situation for fats and oils. In assessing the market pros-
pects for palm oil, some of its physical characteristics have to be taken
into account. Palm oil is a hard oil'; it contains a larger portion of



ANNEX 11
Page 2
saturated fatty acids than most of its major competitors (soybean oil, sun-
flower oil, cottonseed oil, etc.), and unlike the lauric oils (coconut oil
and palm kernel oil), palm oil does not contain any components which would
guarantee it a market in which it is preferred over other fats and oils.
The supply of palm oil is highly inelastic, because harvesting will continue
even if palm oil prices should fall below variable costs. There are two
main reasons: (a) producers have few alternative production possibilities,
short of up-rooting the palms; and (b) they would have to collect the ripe
fresh fruit bunches anyway, to prevent plant diseases caused by rotting
palm fruit. The decision as to whether to extract the oil will depend
largely on the direct operating costs of the mill and the marketing costs
for the end products (palm oil, palm kernnel oil, and palm kernel cake).
6.        The past decade saw a significant shift in the consumption of fats
and oils away from animal fats and oils to vegetable oils. Among the vege-
table oils, the demand for soft oils (soybean oil, cottonseed oil, sunflower
oil, etc.) has expanded more rapidly than the demand for hard oils (palm
oil, coconut oil, palm kernel oil). This trend is expected to continue. By
1985 the share of vegetable oils in total fats and oils consumption will
reach more than 70%. Continued strong demand for soft oils combined with
growing supplies of hard oils will exert increasing pressure on prices for
palm oil and other hard oils. According to Bank projections, world demand
for all fats and oils (based on 1974 constant prices) will rise to 51.7 mil-
lion tons in 1980 and 57.4 million in 1985. Demand for vegetable oils which
has grown at an average rate of 4.2 percent between 1960 and 1975 is ex-
pected to reached about 37 million tons in 1980 and 41 million tons in 1985.
In spite of the advantage of the soft oils, palm oil enjoys a competitive
edge over them because of its low production costs. This is a strong eco-
nomic force which will continue to attract investments in this crop.
7.        During the past decade world production of animal fats and marine
oils has expanded more slowly than the production of vegetable oils. Within
the group of vegetable oils, soybean oil and palm oil showed steepest in-
crease. On the basis of past production trends, world supply of all fats
and oils in 1985 is expected to reaclh 58.3 million tons. Almost one-fourth
of the total supply will come from soybean oil, about 10% from palm oil. The
total share of vegetable oils (hard oils and soft oils combined) will be 70%,
that of animal fats and marine oils, 30%.
8.        Table 7 contains prices for palm oil and other fats and oils for
the period 1960-75; two price indices--one using world exports, the other
using world production as weights--provide an indication of the general price
level of fats and oils. These indices illustrate the dramatic increase in
fats and oils prices which began in 1973 and reached its peak in 1974. In
September 1974 palm oil was traded fcir over US$700 per ton. Then, a 20%



ANNEX 11
Page 3
increase in world soybean production in 1975, combined with good harvests
for most other oilseed crops, led to a sharp decline (25%) of fats and oils
prices. The price for palm oil dropped to US$349 per ton in April 1976.
Because world supply of all fats and oils in 1980 is expected to exceed
world demand by a margin of 1.5 million tons, prices for fats and oils (in
real terms) are expected to be around current low levels. Between 1980 and
1985 demand for fats and oils is projected to grow faster than during 1975-
80, reducing the gap between demand and supplies of fats and oils to 800
thousand tons. Thus prices are projected to recover from their low in 1980
and to approach again their level during the late 1960s.
9.        In Tables 1 to 7 a review is given of world palm oil production,
the main producing, exporting and importing countries, and world production
and exports of selected oil seeds, fats and oils.
10.       Palm oil development in PNG started in 1967.  By 1985 about 23,300 ha
of oil palm (smallholder and nucleus estates) is expected to be in production
(9,000 ha in Hoskins, 9,600 ha in Popondetta, and 4,700 ha in Bialla). Production
of palm oil would amount to 51,000 tons in 1980 and then increase to 103,000
tons in 1985. This would amount to about 1% of total estimated world market
exports of palm oil in 1980 and 2.4% in 1985. Considering these small per-
centages and the overall favorable world market prospects for palm oil pro-
ducts in the eighties, the marketing of PNG's palm oil should not encounter
any difficulties.

ANNEX 12
Page 1
PAPUA NEW GUINEA
POPONDETTA SMALLHOLDER OIL PALM DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Economic Analysis
1.        The economic rate of return of the project has been determined in
accordance with a number of assumptions in respect to the project benefits and
project costs which are quantified in the accompanying table. These assumptions
are enumerated below.
Benefits
2.        Smallholder income has been calculated on the basis of a net FFB price
to the producer of K 22.5 per ton. This assumes palm oil and kernel prices of
US$400 and US$250 per ton cif Europe in constant 1976 prices which is in line
with Bank commodity price projections. It also assumes the various cost and
production coefficients (based on the Hoskins project) which are set out in
Annex 7, Table 2.
3.        In relation to freight savings, the deep-sea wharf facility at Oro
Bay has been described in Annex 5. This facility is identical with the wharf
construction at Kimbe as part of the Hoskins oil palm development project.
Because of increased sea freight turnover as a result of that project, sub-
stantial savings have been achieved because direct overseas cargo shipment
has been possible and been far less costly than trans-shipment via Rabaul.
A similar benefit will undoubtedly accrue to the proposed Popondetta develop-
ment and for the purpose of the appraisal, its quantification has been based
on the following:
(a) annual cargo handling - 21,000 tons (based on actual 1975 tonnage);
(b) without the proposed project, this entire tonnage would be trans-
shipped via Lae at a cost of K 26.30 per ton of which K 7.10
represents wharfage and handling at Oro Bay and Lae and K 2.74
represents stevedoring;
(c) with the proposed project, 33% of cargo currently being tran-
shipped in Lae would be directly shipped into or out of over-
seas ports;
(d) the savings on foreign exchange costs associated with this
transfer would be 75% of K 16.46, i.e. K 12.34 per ton.
4.        No account has been taken of benefits accruing to the economy from
the processing of smallholder FFB production into palm oil and kernels. With-
out accurate details of expected milling costs, these benefits are difficult
to estimate but broadly, they would directly involve:



ANNEX 12
Page 2
(a) the Government's share (50%) of FC net profit
distribution; and
(b) taxation proceeds (corporate and personal) associated
with the operation of the mill facility.
Costs
5.        All development costs except those associated with road works,
include a 5% physical contingency. For road construction and upgrading,
a 20% allowance for physical contingencies has been included.
6.        To obtain a realistic evaluation of the economic return to the
investment in oil palm, it is proposed that the infrastructure development
costs associated with extension services, social services and roads should
not be fully charged to the project. Based on the experience of the Hoskins
project and the demography of the Popondetta area, 75% of the cost of exten-
sion services and 30% of social services have been costed to the project.
With a predominance of public roads in the proposed upgrading and new con-
struction program, no more than 70% of the related development costs should
be charged against the project.
7.        The full minimum rural wage rate (K 1.62 per day) has been used to
determine the financial rate of return for smallholder oil palm development.
However, in calculating the economic rate of return, a shadow wage rate of
K 0.80 per day (reflecting the negligible alternative employment opportunities
in the Popondetta area) has been adopted and the difference of K 0.82 included
in the benefit stream displayed in the accompanying table.
Results
8.        Using the foregoing assumptions and discounting project benefits
and costs over a 31 year period, the economic rate of return is estimated to
be 14%. If all the infrastructure development costs are borne by the project,
then the rate of return would drop to 10.9%. Additional sensitivity tests
have been performed as set out below, covering an overall increase in costs
and a change in benefits as a result of altering world market conditions
for oil palm products and/or FFB yields.
(a)  10% overall cost increase                11.5%
(b)  10% benefit increase                     16.35%
(c)  10% benefit reduction                    11.35%
(d)  combination of (a) and (b)               13.95%
(e)  combination of (a) and (c)                8.85%



ANNEX 12
Page 3
9.        The non-quantifiable benefits of the project, in addition to those
associated with FFB processing (para 4), would include the improved health
and education of settlers' families and the multiplier effects, so apparent
at Kimbe, of local capital formation and increased levels of consumption
expenditure.
10.       These results suggest that by even adopting pessimistic assumptions
as to variations in costs and benefits and at the same time ignoring those
benefits which cannot be quantified, the economic rate of return would still
be adequate to justify the proposed investment.
