ANNEX 13
Page 1
PAPUA NEW GUINEA
POPONDETTA SMALLHOLDER OIL PALM DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Cost and Benefit Recovery
1.        The annex examines the implications for public revenues and
farmers' incomes of the Government's policies towards the recovery of
the capital and recurrent costs incurred in the project. In determining
the extent of cost recovery and the relation of project charges to
benefits, two indices have been used, which are defined as follows for the
present project:
(a) Cost Recovery Index: the ratio of the present worth of in-
cremental charges paid by project farmers to present worth
of incremental project development and operation and mainten-
ance costs; and
(b) Rent Recovery Index: the ratio of incremental charges and
taxes paid by a typical farm family to incremental "project
rent" accruing to the family before paying charges, where
"project rent" is defined as net incremental income less the
value of family labor, returns to management and incremental
investments, and allowances for uncertainty.
The upper limit of the rent recovery index is 100%, but it would normally
be less than that because of political difficulties, tax disincentives,
and costs of collecting taxes. Rent is a difficult concept to measure in
practice, but an attempt is made to determine reasonable quantitative
estimates of its various components based on qualitative considerations.
2.        All charges, costs and benefits are measured at present values
discounted at 10% annual rate of interest over the 31-year evaluation period,
and in terms of 1976 constant prices. Costs and benefits represent financial
flows. The repayment period for loans to farmers includes four years grace,
then 4 to 9 years of repayment at 11% interest. Unless the annual inflation
rate is known over the 31 years, it is impossible to analyze cost and rent
recovery under these terms using current prices. Thus, the cost and rent
recovery indices have been calculated in terms of 1976 constant prices.
3.        In the cost recovery analysis, costs charged against the project
are, 100% of farm development costs, 75% of Extension Services, 70% of Road
Construction and 30% of Social Services since all the benefits are not
captured by project beneficiaries. The present value of these costs dis-
counted at 10% their total net present value is K 9.4 M. The overall pro-
ject cost recovery index is 63%.



ANNEX 13
Page 2
4.         In the rent recovery analysis two farm models are shown; one
for Existing Settlers and one for New Settlers who actually migrate into
the project area and begin from a totally new position. Farm budget data
(Annex 3) are used to derive the net incremental income due to the pro-
ject for each farm group. Incremental family labor was priced at its oppor-
tunity cost of K 0.80/man-day while an allowance of 10% of gross incremental
income was given for value of farmers' management.  The return on farmers' new
investment funds was calculated at 10% of value and an uncertainty allowance
of 10% of gross incremental value of production was made for seasonal variabil-
ity. Under these assumptions and discounting project rent and charges over
the 31 year project life rent recovery was 57% for the new settlers and 74%
for the existing settlers and villagers (Table 1).
5.         There are problems in estimating equitable allowances in these cal-
culations particularly with imputing a value for family labor. If the full
money wage rate of K 1.62/man-day is charged agains the rent then the rent
recovery indices are all above 100%. In these analyses the shadow (economic)
price of labor has been charged at K 0.80. Consequently, these indices must
be regarded as the minimum with "most likely" recovery values somewhat higher
if a more generous allowance is considered appropriate for labor and risk.
6.         These levels of rent recovery are considered satisfactory since
it is not possible to be precise in identifying with any degree of accuracy
the incentive needed to reward the farmers adequately. Secondly, all farmers
do not have the same degree of profitability and some will have a rent re-
covery higher than these. In spite of these comparatively high rent re-
covery indexes the net family income rises from K 615/year to K 1,500/year.
This should be more than adequate incentive to participate.
